Branch of machine learning
For the TV series episode, see [Deep Learning (South Park)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_Learning_\(South_Park\)> "Deep Learning \(South Park\)").

In [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Machine_learning> "Machine learning"), **deep learning** focuses on utilizing multilayered [neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neural_network_\(machine_learning\)> "Neural network \(machine learning\)") to perform tasks such as [classification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Statistical_classification> "Statistical classification"), [regression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Regression_analysis> "Regression analysis"), and [representation learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Representation_learning> "Representation learning"). The field takes inspiration from [biological neuroscience](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neuroscience> "Neuroscience") and is centered around stacking [artificial neurons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Artificial_neuron> "Artificial neuron") into layers and "training" them to process data. The adjective "deep" refers to the use of multiple layers (ranging from three to several hundred or thousands) in the network. Methods used can be [supervised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Supervised_learning> "Supervised learning"), [semi-supervised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Semi-supervised_learning> "Semi-supervised learning") or [unsupervised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Unsupervised_learning> "Unsupervised learning").[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-NatureBengio-2>)
Some common deep learning network architectures include [fully connected networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Fully_connected_network> "Fully connected network"), [deep belief networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_belief_network> "Deep belief network"), [recurrent neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks> "Recurrent neural networks"), [convolutional neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Convolutional_neural_networks> "Convolutional neural networks"), [generative adversarial networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Generative_adversarial_network> "Generative adversarial network"), [transformers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Transformer_\(machine_learning_model\)> "Transformer \(machine learning model\)"), and [neural radiance fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neural_radiance_field> "Neural radiance field"). These architectures have been applied to fields including [computer vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Computer_vision> "Computer vision"), [speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Speech_recognition> "Speech recognition"), [natural language processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Natural_language_processing> "Natural language processing"), [machine translation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Machine_translation> "Machine translation"), [bioinformatics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Bioinformatics> "Bioinformatics"), [drug design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Drug_design> "Drug design"), [medical image analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Medical_image_analysis> "Medical image analysis"), [climate science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Climatology> "Climatology"), material inspection and [board game](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Board_game> "Board game") programs, where they have produced results comparable to and in some cases surpassing human expert performance.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:9-3>)[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-krizhevsky2012-4>)[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-5>)
Early forms of neural networks were inspired by information processing and distributed communication nodes in [biological systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Biological_system> "Biological system"), particularly the [human brain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Human_brain> "Human brain"). However, current neural networks do not intend to model the brain function of organisms, and are generally seen as low-quality models for that purpose.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-6>)
## Overview
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=1> "Edit section: Overview")]
Most modern deep learning models are based on multi-layered [neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neural_network_\(machine_learning\)> "Neural network \(machine learning\)") such as [convolutional neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Convolutional_neural_network> "Convolutional neural network") and [transformers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Transformer_\(neural_network\)> "Transformer \(neural network\)"), although they can also include [propositional formulas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Propositional_formula> "Propositional formula") or latent variables organized layer-wise in deep [generative models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Generative_model> "Generative model") such as the nodes in [deep belief networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_belief_network> "Deep belief network") and deep [Boltzmann machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Boltzmann_machine> "Boltzmann machine").[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIODEEP-7>)
Fundamentally, deep learning refers to a class of [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Machine_learning> "Machine learning") [algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Algorithm> "Algorithm") in which a hierarchy of layers is used to transform input data into a progressively more abstract and composite representation. For example, in an [image recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Image_recognition> "Image recognition") model, the raw input may be an [image](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Image> "Image") (represented as a [tensor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Tensor_\(machine_learning\)> "Tensor \(machine learning\)") of [pixels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Pixel> "Pixel")). The first representational layer may attempt to identify basic shapes such as lines and circles, the second layer may compose and encode arrangements of edges, the third layer may encode a nose and eyes, and the fourth layer may recognize that the image contains a face. 
Importantly, a deep learning process can learn which features to optimally place at which level _on its own_. Prior to deep learning, machine learning techniques often involved hand-crafted [feature engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Feature_engineering> "Feature engineering") to transform the data into a more suitable representation for a classification algorithm to operate on. In the deep learning approach, features are not hand-crafted and the model [discovers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Representation_learning> "Representation learning") useful feature representations from the data automatically. This does not eliminate the need for hand-tuning; for example, varying numbers of layers and layer sizes can provide different degrees of abstraction.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIO2012-8>)[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-NatureBengio-2>)
The word "deep" in "deep learning" refers to the number of layers through which the data is transformed. More precisely, deep learning systems have a substantial _credit assignment path_ (CAP) depth. The CAP is the chain of transformations from input to output. CAPs describe potentially causal connections between input and output. For a [feedforward neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Feedforward_neural_network> "Feedforward neural network"), the depth of the CAPs is that of the network and is the number of hidden layers plus one (as the output layer is also parameterized). For [recurrent neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_network> "Recurrent neural network"), in which a signal may propagate through a layer more than once, the CAP depth is potentially unlimited.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>) No universally agreed-upon threshold of depth divides shallow learning from deep learning, but most researchers agree that deep learning involves CAP depth higher than two. CAP of depth two has been shown to be a universal approximator in the sense that it can emulate any function.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-10>) Beyond that, more layers do not add to the function approximator ability of the network. Deep models (CAP > two) are able to extract better features than shallow models and hence, extra layers help in learning the features effectively. 
Deep learning architectures can be constructed with a [greedy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Greedy_algorithm> "Greedy algorithm") layer-by-layer method.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIO2007-11>) Deep learning helps to disentangle these abstractions and pick out which features improve performance.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIO2012-8>)
Deep learning algorithms can be applied to unsupervised learning tasks. This is an important benefit because unlabeled data is more abundant than the labeled data. Examples of deep structures that can be trained in an unsupervised manner are [deep belief networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_belief_network> "Deep belief network").[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIO2012-8>)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHOLARDBNS-12>)
The term _deep learning_ was introduced to the machine learning community by [Rina Dechter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Rina_Dechter> "Rina Dechter") in 1986,[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-dechter1986-13>) and to artificial neural networks by Igor Aizenberg and colleagues in 2000, in the context of [Boolean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Boolean_network> "Boolean network") threshold neurons.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-MV_1-14>)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-15>) Although the history of its appearance is apparently more complicated.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-16>)
## Interpretations
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=2> "Edit section: Interpretations")]
Deep neural networks are generally interpreted in terms of the [universal approximation theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Universal_approximation_theorem> "Universal approximation theorem")[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-cyb-17>)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-horn-18>)[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Haykin,_Simon_1998-19>)[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Hassoun,_M._1995_p._48-20>)[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ZhouLu-21>) or [probabilistic inference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Bayesian_inference> "Bayesian inference").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-22>)[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BOOK2014-23>)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIO2012-8>)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-MURPHY-24>)
The classic universal approximation theorem concerns the capacity of [feedforward neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Feedforward_neural_networks> "Feedforward neural networks") with a single hidden layer of finite size to approximate [continuous functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Continuous_functions> "Continuous functions").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-cyb-17>)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-horn-18>)[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Haykin,_Simon_1998-19>)[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Hassoun,_M._1995_p._48-20>) In 1989, the first proof was published by [George Cybenko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/George_Cybenko> "George Cybenko") for [sigmoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Sigmoid_function> "Sigmoid function") activation functions[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-cyb-17>) and was generalised to feed-forward multi-layer architectures in 1991 by Kurt Hornik.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-horn-18>) Recent work also showed that universal approximation also holds for non-bounded activation functions such as [Kunihiko Fukushima](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima> "Kunihiko Fukushima")'s [rectified linear unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Rectified_linear_unit> "Rectified linear unit").[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Fukushima1969-25>)[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-sonoda17-26>)
The universal approximation theorem for [deep neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_neural_network> "Deep neural network") concerns the capacity of networks with bounded width but the depth is allowed to grow. Lu et al.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ZhouLu-21>) proved that if the width of a deep neural network with [ReLU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/ReLU> "ReLU") activation is strictly larger than the input dimension, then the network can approximate any [Lebesgue integrable function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Lebesgue_integration> "Lebesgue integration"); if the width is smaller or equal to the input dimension, then a deep neural network is not a universal approximator. 
The [probabilistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Probabilistic> "Probabilistic") interpretation[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-MURPHY-24>) derives from the field of [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Machine_learning> "Machine learning"). It features inference,[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BOOK2014-23>)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIODEEP-7>)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIO2012-8>)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHOLARDBNS-12>)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-MURPHY-24>) as well as the [optimization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Optimization> "Optimization") concepts of [training](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Training> "Training") and [testing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Test_\(assessment\)> "Test \(assessment\)"), related to fitting and [generalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Generalization> "Generalization"), respectively. More specifically, the probabilistic interpretation considers the activation nonlinearity as a [cumulative distribution function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Cumulative_distribution_function> "Cumulative distribution function").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-MURPHY-24>) The probabilistic interpretation led to the introduction of [dropout](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Dropout_\(neural_networks\)> "Dropout \(neural networks\)") as [regularizer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Regularization_\(mathematics\)> "Regularization \(mathematics\)") in neural networks. The probabilistic interpretation was introduced by researchers including [Hopfield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/John_Hopfield> "John Hopfield"), [Widrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Bernard_Widrow> "Bernard Widrow") and [Narendra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Kumpati_S._Narendra> "Kumpati S. Narendra") and popularized in surveys such as the one by [Bishop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Christopher_Bishop> "Christopher Bishop").[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-prml-27>)
## History
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=3> "Edit section: History")]
### Before 1980
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=4> "Edit section: Before 1980")]
There are two [types](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Types_of_artificial_neural_networks> "Types of artificial neural networks") of artificial neural network (ANN): [feedforward neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Feedforward_neural_network> "Feedforward neural network") (FNN) or [multilayer perceptron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Multilayer_perceptron> "Multilayer perceptron") (MLP) and [recurrent neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks> "Recurrent neural networks") (RNN). RNNs have cycles in their connectivity structure, FNNs don't. In the 1920s, [Wilhelm Lenz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Wilhelm_Lenz> "Wilhelm Lenz") and [Ernst Ising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Ernst_Ising> "Ernst Ising") created the [Ising model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Ising_model> "Ising model")[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ising1925-28>)[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-brush67-29>) which is essentially a non-learning RNN architecture consisting of neuron-like threshold elements. In 1972, [Shun'ichi Amari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Shun%27ichi_Amari> "Shun'ichi Amari") made this architecture adaptive.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Amari1972-30>)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>) His learning RNN was republished by [John Hopfield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/John_Hopfield> "John Hopfield") in 1982.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Hopfield1982-32>) Other early [recurrent neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_network> "Recurrent neural network") were published by Kaoru Nakano in 1971.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Nakano1971-33>)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Nakano1972-34>) Already in 1948, [Alan Turing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Alan_Turing> "Alan Turing") produced work on "Intelligent Machinery" that was not published in his lifetime,[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-turing1948-35>) containing "ideas related to artificial evolution and learning RNNs".[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>)
[Frank Rosenblatt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Frank_Rosenblatt> "Frank Rosenblatt") (1958)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-36>) proposed the perceptron, an MLP with 3 layers: an input layer, a hidden layer with randomized weights that did not learn, and an output layer. He later published a 1962 book that also introduced variants and computer experiments, including a version with four-layer perceptrons "with adaptive preterminal networks" where the last two layers have learned weights (here he credits H. D. Block and B. W. Knight).[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-rosenblatt1962-37>): section 16  The book cites an earlier network by R. D. Joseph (1960)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-joseph1960-38>) "functionally equivalent to a variation of" this four-layer system (the book mentions Joseph over 30 times). Should Joseph therefore be considered the originator of proper adaptive [multilayer perceptrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Multilayer_perceptrons> "Multilayer perceptrons") with learning hidden units? Unfortunately, the learning algorithm was not a functional one, and fell into oblivion. 
The first working deep learning algorithm was the [Group method of data handling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Group_method_of_data_handling> "Group method of data handling"), a method to train arbitrarily deep neural networks, published by [Alexey Ivakhnenko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Alexey_Ivakhnenko> "Alexey Ivakhnenko") and Lapa in 1965. They regarded it as a form of polynomial regression,[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ivak1965-39>) or a generalization of Rosenblatt's perceptron.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-40>) A 1971 paper described a deep network with eight layers trained by this method,[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ivak1971-41>) which is based on layer by layer training through regression analysis. Superfluous hidden units are pruned using a separate validation set. Since the activation functions of the nodes are Kolmogorov-Gabor polynomials, these were also the first deep networks with multiplicative units or "gates".[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>)
The first deep learning [multilayer perceptron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Multilayer_perceptron> "Multilayer perceptron") trained by [stochastic gradient descent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Stochastic_gradient_descent> "Stochastic gradient descent")[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-robbins1951-42>) was published in 1967 by [Shun'ichi Amari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Shun%27ichi_Amari> "Shun'ichi Amari").[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Amari1967-43>) In computer experiments conducted by Amari's student Saito, a five layer MLP with two modifiable layers learned [internal representations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Knowledge_representation> "Knowledge representation") to classify non-linearily separable pattern classes.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>) Subsequent developments in hardware and hyperparameter tunings have made end-to-end [stochastic gradient descent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Stochastic_gradient_descent> "Stochastic gradient descent") the currently dominant training technique. 
In 1969, [Kunihiko Fukushima](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima> "Kunihiko Fukushima") introduced the [ReLU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Rectifier_\(neural_networks\)> "Rectifier \(neural networks\)") (rectified linear unit) [activation function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Activation_function> "Activation function").[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Fukushima1969-25>)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>) The rectifier has become the most popular activation function for deep learning.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-44>)
Deep learning architectures for [convolutional neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Convolutional_neural_network> "Convolutional neural network") (CNNs) with convolutional layers and downsampling layers began with the [Neocognitron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neocognitron> "Neocognitron") introduced by [Kunihiko Fukushima](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima> "Kunihiko Fukushima") in 1979, though not trained by backpropagation.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-FUKU1979-45>)[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-FUKU1980-46>)
[Backpropagation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Backpropagation> "Backpropagation") is an efficient application of the [chain rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Chain_rule> "Chain rule") derived by [Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz> "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") in 1673[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-leibniz1676-47>) to networks of differentiable nodes. The terminology "back-propagating errors" was actually introduced in 1962 by Rosenblatt,[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-rosenblatt1962-37>) but he did not know how to implement this, although [Henry J. Kelley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Henry_J._Kelley> "Henry J. Kelley") had a continuous precursor of backpropagation in 1960 in the context of [control theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Control_theory> "Control theory").[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-kelley1960-48>) The modern form of backpropagation was first published in [Seppo Linnainmaa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Seppo_Linnainmaa> "Seppo Linnainmaa")'s master thesis (1970).[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-lin19703-49>)[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-lin19763-50>)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>) G.M. Ostrovski et al. republished it in 1971.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ostrowski1971-51>)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-backprop-52>) [Paul Werbos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Paul_Werbos> "Paul Werbos") applied backpropagation to neural networks in 1982[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-werbos1982-53>) (his 1974 PhD thesis, reprinted in a 1994 book,[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-werbos1974-54>) did not yet describe the algorithm[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-backprop-52>)). In 1986, [David E. Rumelhart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/David_E._Rumelhart> "David E. Rumelhart") et al. popularised backpropagation but did not cite the original work.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-55>)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-rumelhart1986-56>)
### 1980s-2000s
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=5> "Edit section: 1980s-2000s")]
The [time delay neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Time_delay_neural_network> "Time delay neural network") (TDNN) was introduced in 1987 by [Alex Waibel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Alex_Waibel> "Alex Waibel") to apply CNN to phoneme recognition. It used convolutions, weight sharing, and backpropagation.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Waibel1987-57>)[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-speechsignal-58>) In 1988, Wei Zhang applied a backpropagation-trained CNN to alphabet recognition.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-wz1988-59>) In 1989, [Yann LeCun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Yann_LeCun> "Yann LeCun") et al. created a CNN called [LeNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/LeNet> "LeNet") for [recognizing handwritten ZIP codes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Handwriting_recognition> "Handwriting recognition") on mail. Training required 3 days.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-LECUN1989-60>) In 1990, Wei Zhang implemented a CNN on [optical computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Optical_computing> "Optical computing") hardware.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-wz1990-61>) In 1991, a CNN was applied to medical image object segmentation[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-62>) and breast cancer detection in mammograms.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-63>) [LeNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/LeNet> "LeNet")-5 (1998), a 7-level CNN by [Yann LeCun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Yann_LeCun> "Yann LeCun") et al., that classifies digits, was applied by several banks to recognize hand-written numbers on checks digitized in 32x32 pixel images.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-lecun98-64>)
[Recurrent neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_network> "Recurrent neural network") (RNN)[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ising1925-28>)[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Amari1972-30>) were further developed in the 1980s. Recurrence is used for sequence processing, and when a recurrent network is unrolled, it mathematically resembles a deep feedforward layer. Consequently, they have similar properties and issues, and their developments had mutual influences. In RNN, two early influential works were the [Jordan network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_network#Jordan_network> "Recurrent neural network") (1986)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-65>) and the [Elman network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_network#Elman_network> "Recurrent neural network") (1990),[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-66>) which applied RNN to study problems in [cognitive psychology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Cognitive_psychology> "Cognitive psychology"). 
In the 1980s, backpropagation did not work well for deep learning with long credit assignment paths. To overcome this problem, in 1991, [Jürgen Schmidhuber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Schmidhuber> "Jürgen Schmidhuber") proposed a hierarchy of RNNs pre-trained one level at a time by [self-supervised learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Self-supervised_learning> "Self-supervised learning") where each RNN tries to predict its own next input, which is the next unexpected input of the RNN below.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-chunker1991-67>)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-schmidhuber1992-68>) This "neural history compressor" uses [predictive coding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Predictive_coding> "Predictive coding") to learn [internal representations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Knowledge_representation> "Knowledge representation") at multiple self-organizing time scales. This can substantially facilitate downstream deep learning. The RNN hierarchy can be _collapsed_ into a single RNN, by [distilling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Knowledge_distillation> "Knowledge distillation") a higher level _chunker_ network into a lower level _automatizer_ network.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-chunker1991-67>)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-schmidhuber1992-68>)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DLhistory-31>) In 1993, a neural history compressor solved a "Very Deep Learning" task that required more than 1000 subsequent [layers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Layer_\(deep_learning\)> "Layer \(deep learning\)") in an RNN unfolded in time.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-schmidhuber1993-69>) The "P" in [ChatGPT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/ChatGPT> "ChatGPT") refers to such pre-training. 
[Sepp Hochreiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Sepp_Hochreiter> "Sepp Hochreiter")'s diploma thesis (1991)[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HOCH1991-70>) implemented the neural history compressor,[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-chunker1991-67>) and identified and analyzed the [vanishing gradient problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Vanishing_gradient_problem> "Vanishing gradient problem").[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HOCH1991-70>)[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HOCH2001-71>) Hochreiter proposed recurrent [residual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Residual_neural_network> "Residual neural network") connections to solve the vanishing gradient problem. This led to the [long short-term memory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Long_short-term_memory> "Long short-term memory") (LSTM), published in 1995.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-72>) LSTM can learn "very deep learning" tasks[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>) with long credit assignment paths that require memories of events that happened thousands of discrete time steps before. That LSTM was not yet the modern architecture, which required a "forget gate", introduced in 1999,[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-lstm1999-73>) which became the standard RNN architecture. 
In 1991, [Jürgen Schmidhuber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Schmidhuber> "Jürgen Schmidhuber") also published adversarial neural networks that contest with each other in the form of a [zero-sum game](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Zero-sum_game> "Zero-sum game"), where one network's gain is the other network's loss.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-curiosity1991-74>)[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-fun2010-75>) The first network is a [generative model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Generative_model> "Generative model") that models a [probability distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Probability_distribution> "Probability distribution") over output patterns. The second network learns by [gradient descent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Gradient_descent> "Gradient descent") to predict the reactions of the environment to these patterns. This was called "artificial curiosity". In 2014, this principle was used in [generative adversarial networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Generative_adversarial_network> "Generative adversarial network") (GANs).[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-gancurpm2020-76>)
During 1985–1995, inspired by statistical mechanics, several architectures and methods were developed by [Terry Sejnowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Terry_Sejnowski> "Terry Sejnowski"), [Peter Dayan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Peter_Dayan> "Peter Dayan"), [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton> "Geoffrey Hinton"), etc., including the [Boltzmann machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Boltzmann_machine> "Boltzmann machine"),[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-77>) [restricted Boltzmann machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Restricted_Boltzmann_machine> "Restricted Boltzmann machine"),[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-78>) [Helmholtz machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Helmholtz_machine> "Helmholtz machine"),[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-nc95-79>) and the [wake-sleep algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Wake-sleep_algorithm> "Wake-sleep algorithm").[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:1-80>) These were designed for unsupervised learning of deep generative models. However, those were more computationally expensive compared to backpropagation. Boltzmann machine learning algorithm, published in 1985, was briefly popular before being eclipsed by the backpropagation algorithm in 1986. (p. 112 [[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-81>)). A 1988 network became state of the art in [protein structure prediction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Protein_structure_prediction> "Protein structure prediction"), an early application of deep learning to bioinformatics.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-82>)
Both shallow and deep learning (e.g., recurrent nets) of ANNs for [speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Speech_recognition> "Speech recognition") have been explored for many years.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-83>)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Robinson1992-84>)[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-85>) These methods never outperformed non-uniform internal-handcrafting Gaussian [mixture model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Mixture_model> "Mixture model")/[Hidden Markov model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Hidden_Markov_model> "Hidden Markov model") (GMM-HMM) technology based on generative models of speech trained discriminatively.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Baker2009-86>) Key difficulties have been analyzed, including gradient diminishing[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HOCH1991-70>) and weak temporal correlation structure in neural predictive models.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Bengio1991-87>)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Deng1994-88>) Additional difficulties were the lack of training data and limited computing power. 
Most [speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Speech_recognition> "Speech recognition") researchers moved away from neural nets to pursue generative modeling. An exception was at [SRI International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/SRI_International> "SRI International") in the late 1990s. Funded by the US government's [NSA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/National_Security_Agency> "National Security Agency") and [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/DARPA> "DARPA"), SRI researched in speech and [speaker recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Speaker_recognition> "Speaker recognition"). The speaker recognition team led by [Larry Heck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Larry_Heck> "Larry Heck") reported significant success with deep neural networks in speech processing in the 1998 [NIST](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology> "National Institute of Standards and Technology") Speaker Recognition benchmark.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Doddington2000-89>)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Heck2000-90>) It was deployed in the Nuance Verifier, representing the first major industrial application of deep learning.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-91>)
The principle of elevating "raw" features over hand-crafted optimization was first explored successfully in the architecture of deep autoencoder on the "raw" spectrogram or linear [filter-bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Filter_bank> "Filter bank") features in the late 1990s,[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Heck2000-90>) showing its superiority over the [Mel-Cepstral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Mel-frequency_cepstrum> "Mel-frequency cepstrum") features that contain stages of fixed transformation from spectrograms. The raw features of speech, [waveforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Waveform> "Waveform"), later produced excellent larger-scale results.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-92>)
### 2000s
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=6> "Edit section: 2000s")]
Neural networks entered a lull, and simpler models that use task-specific handcrafted features such as [Gabor filters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Gabor_filter> "Gabor filter") and [support vector machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Support_vector_machine> "Support vector machine") (SVMs) became the preferred choices in the 1990s and 2000s, because of artificial neural networks' computational cost and a lack of understanding of how the brain wires its biological networks.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed> "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]
In 2003, LSTM became competitive with traditional speech recognizers on certain tasks.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-graves2003-93>) In 2006, [Alex Graves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Alex_Graves_\(computer_scientist\)> "Alex Graves \(computer scientist\)"), Santiago Fernández, Faustino Gomez, and Schmidhuber combined it with [connectionist temporal classification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Connectionist_temporal_classification> "Connectionist temporal classification") (CTC)[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-graves2006-94>) in stacks of LSTMs.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-fernandez2007keyword-95>) In 2009, it became the first RNN to win a [pattern recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Pattern_recognition> "Pattern recognition") contest, in connected [handwriting recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Handwriting_recognition> "Handwriting recognition").[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-96>)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>)
In 2006, publications by [Geoff Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton> "Geoffrey Hinton"), [Ruslan Salakhutdinov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Russ_Salakhutdinov> "Russ Salakhutdinov"), Osindero and [Teh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Yee_Whye_Teh> "Yee Whye Teh")[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-97>)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-hinton06-98>) [deep belief networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_belief_network> "Deep belief network") were developed for generative modeling. They are trained by training one restricted Boltzmann machine, then freezing it and training another one on top of the first one, and so on, then optionally [fine-tuned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Fine-tuning_\(deep_learning\)> "Fine-tuning \(deep learning\)") using supervised backpropagation.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HINTON2007-99>) They could model high-dimensional probability distributions, such as the distribution of [MNIST images](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/MNIST_database> "MNIST database"), but convergence was slow.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-100>)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-101>)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-102>)
The impact of deep learning in industry began in the early 2000s, when CNNs already processed an estimated 10% to 20% of all the checks written in the US, according to Yann LeCun.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-lecun2016slides-103>) Industrial applications of deep learning to large-scale speech recognition started around 2010. 
The 2009 NIPS Workshop on Deep Learning for Speech Recognition was motivated by the limitations of deep generative models of speech, and the possibility that given more capable hardware and large-scale data sets that deep neural nets might become practical. It was believed that pre-training DNNs using generative models of deep belief nets (DBN) would overcome the main difficulties of neural nets. However, it was discovered that replacing pre-training with large amounts of training data for straightforward backpropagation when using DNNs with large, context-dependent output layers produced error rates dramatically lower than then-state-of-the-art Gaussian mixture model (GMM)/Hidden Markov Model (HMM) and also than more-advanced generative model-based systems.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HintonDengYu2012-104>) The nature of the recognition errors produced by the two types of systems was characteristically different,[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ReferenceICASSP2013-105>) offering technical insights into how to integrate deep learning into the existing highly efficient, run-time speech decoding system deployed by all major speech recognition systems.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BOOK2014-23>)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ReferenceA-106>)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-107>) Analysis around 2009–2010, contrasting the GMM (and other generative speech models) vs. DNN models, stimulated early industrial investment in deep learning for speech recognition.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ReferenceICASSP2013-105>) That analysis was done with comparable performance (less than 1.5% in error rate) between discriminative DNNs and generative models.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HintonDengYu2012-104>)[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ReferenceICASSP2013-105>)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-interspeech2014Keynote-108>) In 2010, researchers extended deep learning from [TIMIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/TIMIT> "TIMIT") to large vocabulary speech recognition, by adopting large output layers of the DNN based on context-dependent HMM states constructed by [decision trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Decision_tree> "Decision tree").[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Roles2010-109>)[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-110>)[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-111>)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ReferenceA-106>)
### Deep learning revolution
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=7> "Edit section: Deep learning revolution")]
[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/AI-ML-DL.svg/250px-AI-ML-DL.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/File:AI-ML-DL.svg>)How deep learning is a subset of machine learning and how machine learning is a subset of artificial intelligence (AI)
The deep learning revolution started around CNN- and GPU-based computer vision. 
Although CNNs trained by backpropagation had been around for decades and GPU implementations of NNs for years,[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-jung2004-112>) including CNNs,[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-chellapilla2006-113>) faster implementations of CNNs on GPUs were needed to progress on computer vision. Later, as deep learning becomes widespread, specialized hardware and algorithm optimizations were developed specifically for deep learning.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-sze2017-114>)
A key advance for the deep learning revolution was hardware advances, especially GPU. Some early work dated back to 2004.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-jung2004-112>)[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-chellapilla2006-113>) In 2009, Raina, Madhavan, and [Andrew Ng](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Andrew_Ng> "Andrew Ng") reported a 100M deep belief network trained on 30 Nvidia [GeForce GTX 280](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/GeForce_GTX_280> "GeForce GTX 280") GPUs, an early demonstration of GPU-based deep learning. They reported up to 70 times faster training.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-115>)
In 2011, a CNN named _DanNet[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:3-116>)_[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:6-117>) by Dan Ciresan, Ueli Meier, Jonathan Masci, [Luca Maria Gambardella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Luca_Maria_Gambardella> "Luca Maria Gambardella"), and [Jürgen Schmidhuber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Schmidhuber> "Jürgen Schmidhuber") achieved for the first time superhuman performance in a visual pattern recognition contest, outperforming traditional methods by a factor of 3.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>) It then won more contests.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:8-118>)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ciresan2013miccai-119>) They also showed how [max-pooling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Max_pooling> "Max pooling") CNNs on GPU improved performance significantly.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:9-3>)
In 2012, [Andrew Ng](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Andrew_Ng> "Andrew Ng") and [Jeff Dean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Jeff_Dean_\(computer_scientist\)> "Jeff Dean \(computer scientist\)") created an FNN that learned to recognize higher-level concepts, such as cats, only from watching unlabeled images taken from [YouTube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/YouTube> "YouTube") videos.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ng2012-120>)
In October 2012, [AlexNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/AlexNet> "AlexNet") by [Alex Krizhevsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Alex_Krizhevsky> "Alex Krizhevsky"), [Ilya Sutskever](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Ilya_Sutskever> "Ilya Sutskever"), and [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton> "Geoffrey Hinton")[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-krizhevsky2012-4>) won the large-scale [ImageNet competition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/ImageNet_competition> "ImageNet competition") by a significant margin over shallow machine learning methods. Further incremental improvements included the [VGG-16](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/VGG-19> "VGG-19") network by [Karen Simonyan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Karen_Simonyan_\(scientist\)&action=edit&redlink=1> "Karen Simonyan \(scientist\) \(page does not exist\)") and [Andrew Zisserman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Andrew_Zisserman> "Andrew Zisserman")[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-VGG-121>) and Google's [Inceptionv3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Inceptionv3> "Inceptionv3").[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-szegedy-122>)
The success in image classification was then extended to the more challenging task of [generating descriptions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Automatic_image_annotation> "Automatic image annotation") (captions) for images, often as a combination of CNNs and LSTMs.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-1411.4555-123>)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-1411.4952-124>)[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-1411.2539-125>)
In 2014, the state of the art was training “very deep neural network” with 20 to 30 layers.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-126>) Stacking too many layers led to a steep reduction in [training](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Training,_validation,_and_test_data_sets> "Training, validation, and test data sets") accuracy,[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-prelu-127>) known as the "degradation" problem.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-resnet-128>) In 2015, two techniques were developed to train very deep networks: the Highway Network was published in May 2015, and the [residual neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Residual_neural_network> "Residual neural network") (ResNet)[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-resnet20152-129>) in Dec 2015. ResNet behaves like an open-gated Highway Net. 
Around the same time, deep learning started impacting the field of art. Early examples included [Google DeepDream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/DeepDream> "DeepDream") (2015), and [neural style transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neural_style_transfer> "Neural style transfer") (2015),[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-130>) both of which were based on pretrained image classification neural networks, such as [VGG-19](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/VGG-19> "VGG-19"). 
[Generative adversarial network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Generative_adversarial_network> "Generative adversarial network") (GAN) by ([Ian Goodfellow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Ian_Goodfellow> "Ian Goodfellow") et al., 2014)[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-GANnips-131>) (based on [Jürgen Schmidhuber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Schmidhuber> "Jürgen Schmidhuber")'s principle of artificial curiosity[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-curiosity1991-74>)[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-gancurpm2020-76>)) became state of the art in generative modeling during 2014-2018 period. Excellent image quality is achieved by [Nvidia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Nvidia> "Nvidia")'s [StyleGAN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/StyleGAN> "StyleGAN") (2018)[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SyncedReview2018-132>) based on the Progressive GAN by Tero Karras et al.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-progressiveGAN2017-133>) Here the GAN generator is grown from small to large scale in a pyramidal fashion. Image generation by GAN reached popular success, and provoked discussions concerning [deepfakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deepfake> "Deepfake").[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-134>) [Diffusion models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Diffusion_model> "Diffusion model") (2015)[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-135>) eclipsed GANs in generative modeling since then, with systems such as [DALL·E 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/DALL-E> "DALL-E") (2022) and [Stable Diffusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Stable_Diffusion> "Stable Diffusion") (2022). 
In 2015, Google's speech recognition improved by 49% by an LSTM-based model, which they made available through [Google Voice Search](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Google_Voice_Search> "Google Voice Search") on [smartphone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Smartphone> "Smartphone").[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-GoogleVoiceTranscription-136>)[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-sak2015-137>)
Deep learning is part of state-of-the-art systems in various disciplines, particularly computer vision and [automatic speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Automatic_speech_recognition> "Automatic speech recognition") (ASR). Results on commonly used evaluation sets such as [TIMIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/TIMIT> "TIMIT") (ASR) and [MNIST](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/MNIST_database> "MNIST database") ([image classification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Image_classification> "Image classification")), as well as a range of large-vocabulary speech recognition tasks have steadily improved.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HintonDengYu2012-104>)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-138>) Convolutional neural networks were superseded for ASR by [LSTM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/LSTM> "LSTM").[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-sak2015-137>)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-sak2014-139>)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-liwu2015-140>)[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-zen2015-141>) but are more successful in computer vision. 
[Yoshua Bengio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Yoshua_Bengio> "Yoshua Bengio"), [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton> "Geoffrey Hinton") and [Yann LeCun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Yann_LeCun> "Yann LeCun") were awarded the 2018 [Turing Award](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Turing_Award> "Turing Award") for "conceptual and engineering breakthroughs that have made deep neural networks a critical component of computing".[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-142>)
## Neural networks
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=8> "Edit section: Neural networks")]
Main article: [Artificial neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Artificial_neural_network> "Artificial neural network")
[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simplified_neural_network_training_example.svg/250px-Simplified_neural_network_training_example.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/File:Simplified_neural_network_training_example.svg>)
Simplified example of training a neural network in object detection: The network is trained by multiple images that are known to depict [starfish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Starfish> "Starfish") and [sea urchins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Sea_urchin> "Sea urchin"), which are correlated with "nodes" that represent visual [features](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Feature_\(computer_vision\)> "Feature \(computer vision\)"). The starfish match with a ringed texture and a star outline, whereas most sea urchins match with a striped texture and oval shape. However, the instance of a ring textured sea urchin creates a weakly weighted association between them.
[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Simplified_neural_network_example.svg/250px-Simplified_neural_network_example.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/File:Simplified_neural_network_example.svg>)
Subsequent run of the network on an input image (left):[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-143>) The network correctly detects the starfish. However, the weakly weighted association between ringed texture and sea urchin also confers a weak signal to the latter from one of two intermediate nodes. In addition, a [shell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Seashell> "Seashell") that was not included in the training gives a weak signal for the oval shape, also resulting in a weak signal for the sea urchin output. These weak signals may result in a [false positive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/False_positive> "False positive") result for sea urchin.In reality, textures and outlines would not be represented by single nodes, but rather by associated weight patterns of multiple nodes.
**Artificial neural networks** (**ANNs**) or **[connectionist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Connectionism> "Connectionism") systems** are computing systems inspired by the [biological neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Biological_neural_network> "Biological neural network") that constitute animal brains. Such systems learn (progressively improve their ability) to do tasks by considering examples, generally without task-specific programming. For example, in image recognition, they might learn to identify images that contain cats by analyzing example images that have been manually [labeled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Labeled_data> "Labeled data") as "cat" or "no cat" and using the analytic results to identify cats in other images. They have found most use in applications difficult to express with a traditional computer algorithm using [rule-based programming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Rule-based_programming> "Rule-based programming"). 
An ANN is based on a collection of connected units called [artificial neurons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Artificial_neuron> "Artificial neuron"), (analogous to biological [neurons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Neuron> "Neuron") in a [biological brain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Brain> "Brain")). Each connection ([synapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Synapse> "Synapse")) between neurons can transmit a signal to another neuron. The receiving (postsynaptic) neuron can process the signal(s) and then signal downstream neurons connected to it. Neurons may have state, generally represented by [real numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Real_numbers> "Real numbers"), typically between 0 and 1. Neurons and synapses may also have a weight that varies as learning proceeds, which can increase or decrease the strength of the signal that it sends downstream. 
Typically, neurons are organized in layers. Different layers may perform different kinds of transformations on their inputs. Signals travel from the first (input), to the last (output) layer, possibly after traversing the layers multiple times. 
The original goal of the neural network approach was to solve problems in the same way that a human brain would. Over time, attention focused on matching specific mental abilities, leading to deviations from biology such as [backpropagation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Backpropagation> "Backpropagation"), or passing information in the reverse direction and adjusting the network to reflect that information. 
Neural networks have been used on a variety of tasks, including computer vision, [speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Speech_recognition> "Speech recognition"), [machine translation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Machine_translation> "Machine translation"), [social network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Social_network> "Social network") filtering, [playing board and video games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/General_game_playing> "General game playing") and medical diagnosis. 
As of 2017, neural networks typically have a few thousand to a few million units and millions of connections. Despite this number being several order of magnitude less than the number of neurons on a human brain, these networks can perform many tasks at a level beyond that of humans (e.g., recognizing faces, or playing "Go"[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-144>)). 
### Deep neural networks
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=9> "Edit section: Deep neural networks")]
A deep neural network (DNN) is an artificial neural network with multiple layers between the input and output layers.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIODEEP-7>)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>) There are different types of neural networks but they always consist of the same components: neurons, synapses, weights, biases, and functions.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Nokkada-145>) These components as a whole function in a way that mimics functions of the human brain, and can be trained like any other ML algorithm.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed> "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]
For example, a DNN that is trained to recognize dog breeds will go over the given image and calculate the probability that the dog in the image is a certain breed. The user can review the results and select which probabilities the network should display (above a certain threshold, etc.) and return the proposed label. Each mathematical manipulation as such is considered a layer, [[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Kumar2021-146>) and complex DNN have many layers, hence the name "deep" networks. 
DNNs can model complex non-linear relationships. DNN architectures generate compositional models where the object is expressed as a layered composition of [primitives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Primitive_data_type> "Primitive data type").[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-147>) The extra layers enable composition of features from lower layers, potentially modeling complex data with fewer units than a similarly performing shallow network.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BENGIODEEP-7>) For instance, it was proved that sparse [multivariate polynomials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Multivariate_polynomial> "Multivariate polynomial") are exponentially easier to approximate with DNNs than with shallow networks.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-rolnickpaper-148>)
Deep architectures include many variants of a few basic approaches. Each architecture has found success in specific domains. It is not always possible to compare the performance of multiple architectures, unless they have been evaluated on the same data sets.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Kumar2021-146>)
DNNs are typically feedforward networks in which data flows from the input layer to the output layer without looping back. At first, the DNN creates a map of virtual neurons and assigns random numerical values, or "weights", to connections between them. The weights and inputs are multiplied and return an output between 0 and 1. If the network did not accurately recognize a particular pattern, an algorithm would adjust the weights.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-149>) That way the algorithm can make certain parameters more influential, until it determines the correct mathematical manipulation to fully process the data. 
[Recurrent neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks> "Recurrent neural networks"), in which data can flow in any direction, are used for applications such as [language modeling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Language_model> "Language model").[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-gers2001-150>)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-NIPS2014-151>)[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-vinyals2016-152>)[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-gillick2015-153>)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-MIKO2010-154>) Long short-term memory is particularly effective for this use.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:0-155>)[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:10-156>)
[Convolutional neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Convolutional_neural_network> "Convolutional neural network") (CNNs) are used in computer vision.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-LECUN86-157>) CNNs also have been applied to [acoustic modeling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Acoustic_model> "Acoustic model") for automatic speech recognition (ASR).[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:2-158>)
#### Challenges
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=10> "Edit section: Challenges")]
As with ANNs, many issues can arise with naively trained DNNs. Two common issues are [overfitting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Overfitting> "Overfitting") and computation time. 
DNNs are prone to overfitting because of the added layers of abstraction, which allow them to model rare dependencies in the training data. [Regularization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Regularization_\(mathematics\)> "Regularization \(mathematics\)") methods such as Ivakhnenko's unit pruning[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ivak1971-41>) or [weight decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Weight_decay> "Weight decay") ( ℓ 2 {\displaystyle \ell _{2}} ![{\\displaystyle \\ell _{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/85a4571ee9be10bd3c9df2480ab3d280f99e801a)-regularization) or [sparsity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Sparse_matrix> "Sparse matrix") ( ℓ 1 {\displaystyle \ell _{1}} ![{\\displaystyle \\ell _{1}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/361ddd720474aa41cb05453e03424fb7999d3b02)-regularization) can be applied during training to combat overfitting.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-159>) Alternatively [dropout](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Dropout_\(neural_networks\)> "Dropout \(neural networks\)") regularization randomly omits units from the hidden layers during training. This helps to exclude rare dependencies.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-DAHL2013-160>) Another interesting recent development is research into models of just enough complexity through an estimation of the intrinsic complexity of the task being modelled. This approach has been successfully applied for multivariate time series prediction tasks such as traffic prediction.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Kumar2024-161>) Finally, data can be augmented via methods such as cropping and rotating such that smaller training sets can be increased in size to reduce the chances of overfitting.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-162>)
DNNs must consider many training parameters, such as the size (number of layers and number of units per layer), the [learning rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Learning_rate> "Learning rate"), and initial weights. [Sweeping through the parameter space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Hyperparameter_optimization#Grid_search> "Hyperparameter optimization") for optimal parameters may not be feasible due to the cost in time and computational resources. Various tricks, such as [batching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Batch_learning> "Batch learning") (computing the gradient on several training examples at once rather than individual examples)[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-RBMTRAIN-163>) speed up computation. Large processing capabilities of many-core architectures (such as GPUs or the Intel Xeon Phi) have produced significant speedups in training, because of the suitability of such processing architectures for the matrix and vector computations.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-164>)[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-165>)
Alternatively, engineers may look for other types of neural networks with more straightforward and convergent training algorithms. CMAC ([cerebellar model articulation controller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Cerebellar_model_articulation_controller> "Cerebellar model articulation controller")) is one such kind of neural network. It doesn't require learning rates or randomized initial weights. The training process can be guaranteed to converge in one step with a new batch of data, and the computational complexity of the training algorithm is linear with respect to the number of neurons involved.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Qin1-166>)[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Qin2-167>)
## Hardware
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=11> "Edit section: Hardware")]
Since the 2010s, advances in both machine learning algorithms and [computer hardware](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Computer_hardware> "Computer hardware") have led to more efficient methods for training deep neural networks that contain many layers of non-linear hidden units and a very large output layer.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-168>) By 2019, [graphics processing units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Graphics_processing_unit> "Graphics processing unit") (GPUs), often with AI-specific enhancements, had displaced CPUs as the dominant method for training large-scale commercial cloud AI .[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-169>) [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/OpenAI> "OpenAI") estimated the hardware computation used in the largest deep learning projects from AlexNet (2012) to AlphaZero (2017) and found a 300,000-fold increase in the amount of computation required, with a doubling-time trendline of 3.4 months.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-170>)[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-171>)
Special [electronic circuits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Electronic_circuit> "Electronic circuit") called [deep learning processors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Deep_learning_processor> "Deep learning processor") were designed to speed up deep learning algorithms. Deep learning processors include neural processing units (NPUs) in [Huawei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Huawei> "Huawei") cellphones[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-172>) and [cloud computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Cloud_computing> "Cloud computing") servers such as [tensor processing units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Tensor_processing_unit> "Tensor processing unit") (TPU) in the [Google Cloud Platform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Google_Cloud_Platform> "Google Cloud Platform").[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-173>) [Cerebras Systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Cerebras> "Cerebras") has also built a dedicated system to handle large deep learning models, the CS-2, based on the largest processor in the industry, the second-generation Wafer Scale Engine (WSE-2).[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-174>)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-175>)
Atomically thin [semiconductors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Semiconductors> "Semiconductors") are considered promising for energy-efficient deep learning hardware where the same basic device structure is used for both logic operations and data storage. In 2020, Marega et al. published experiments with a large-area active channel material for developing logic-in-memory devices and circuits based on [floating-gate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Floating-gate> "Floating-gate") [field-effect transistors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Field-effect_transistor> "Field-effect transistor") (FGFETs).[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-atomthin-176>)
In 2021, J. Feldmann et al. proposed an integrated [photonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Photonic> "Photonic") [hardware accelerator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Hardware_accelerator> "Hardware accelerator") for parallel convolutional processing.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-photonic-177>) The authors identify two key advantages of integrated photonics over its electronic counterparts: (1) massively parallel data transfer through [wavelength](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Wavelength> "Wavelength") division [multiplexing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Multiplexing> "Multiplexing") in conjunction with [frequency combs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Frequency_comb> "Frequency comb"), and (2) extremely high data modulation speeds.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-photonic-177>) Their system can execute trillions of multiply-accumulate operations per second, indicating the potential of [integrated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Photonic_integrated_circuit> "Photonic integrated circuit") [photonics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Photonics> "Photonics") in data-heavy AI applications.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-photonic-177>)
## Applications
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=12> "Edit section: Applications")]
### Automatic speech recognition
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=13> "Edit section: Automatic speech recognition")]
Main article: [Speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Speech_recognition> "Speech recognition")
Large-scale automatic speech recognition is the first and most convincing successful case of deep learning. LSTM RNNs can learn "Very Deep Learning" tasks[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-SCHIDHUB-9>) that involve multi-second intervals containing speech events separated by thousands of discrete time steps, where one time step corresponds to about 10 ms. LSTM with forget gates[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:10-156>) is competitive with traditional speech recognizers on certain tasks.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-graves2003-93>)
The initial success in speech recognition was based on small-scale recognition tasks based on TIMIT. The data set contains 630 speakers from eight major [dialects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Dialect> "Dialect") of [American English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/American_English> "American English"), where each speaker reads 10 sentences.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-APC_1-178>) Its small size lets many configurations be tried. More importantly, the TIMIT task concerns [phone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Phone_\(phonetics\)> "Phone \(phonetics\)")-sequence recognition, which, unlike word-sequence recognition, allows weak phone [bigram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Bigram> "Bigram") language models. This lets the strength of the acoustic modeling aspects of speech recognition be more easily analyzed. The error rates listed below, including these early results and measured as percent phone error rates (PER), have been summarized since 1991. 
Method | Percent phoneerror rate (PER) (%)   
---|---  
Randomly Initialized RNN[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-179>) | 26.1   
Bayesian Triphone GMM-HMM | 25.6   
Hidden Trajectory (Generative) Model | 24.8   
Monophone Randomly Initialized DNN | 23.4   
Monophone DBN-DNN | 22.4   
Triphone GMM-HMM with BMMI Training | 21.7   
Monophone DBN-DNN on fbank | 20.7   
Convolutional DNN[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-CNN-2014-180>) | 20.0   
Convolutional DNN w. Heterogeneous Pooling | 18.7   
Ensemble DNN/CNN/RNN[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-EnsembleDL-181>) | 18.3   
Bidirectional LSTM | 17.8   
Hierarchical Convolutional Deep Maxout Network[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-HCDMM-182>) | 16.5   
The debut of DNNs for speaker recognition in the late 1990s and speech recognition around 2009-2011 and of LSTM around 2003–2007, accelerated progress in eight major areas:[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BOOK2014-23>)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-interspeech2014Keynote-108>)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-ReferenceA-106>)
  * Scale-up/out and accelerated DNN training and decoding
  * Sequence discriminative training
  * Feature processing by deep models with solid understanding of the underlying mechanisms
  * Adaptation of DNNs and related deep models
  * [Multi-task](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Multi-task_learning> "Multi-task learning") and [transfer learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Transfer_learning> "Transfer learning") by DNNs and related deep models
  * [CNNs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Convolutional_neural_network> "Convolutional neural network") and how to design them to best exploit [domain knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Domain_knowledge> "Domain knowledge") of speech
  * [RNN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Recurrent_neural_network> "Recurrent neural network") and its rich LSTM variants
  * Other types of deep models including tensor-based models and integrated deep generative/discriminative models.


All major commercial speech recognition systems (e.g., Microsoft [Cortana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Cortana_\(software\)> "Cortana \(software\)"), [Xbox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Xbox> "Xbox"), [Skype Translator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Skype_Translator> "Skype Translator"), [Amazon Alexa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Amazon_Alexa> "Amazon Alexa"), [Google Now](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Google_Now> "Google Now"), [Apple Siri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Siri> "Siri"), [Baidu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Baidu> "Baidu") and [iFlyTek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/IFlytek> "IFlytek") voice search, and a range of [Nuance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Nuance_Communications> "Nuance Communications") speech products, etc.) are based on deep learning.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-BOOK2014-23>)[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-183>)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-Baidu-184>)
### Image recognition
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=14> "Edit section: Image recognition")]
Main article: [Computer vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Computer_vision> "Computer vision")
Richard Green explains how deep learning is used with a [remotely operated vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Remotely_operated_underwater_vehicle> "Remotely operated underwater vehicle") in [mussel aquaculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/Mussel#Aquaculture> "Mussel").
A common evaluation set for image classification is the [MNIST database](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</wiki/MNIST_database> "MNIST database") data set. MNIST is composed of handwritten digits and includes 60,000 training examples and 10,000 test examples. As with TIMIT, its small size lets users test multiple configurations. A comprehensive list of results on this set is available.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-YANNMNIST-185>)
Deep learning-based image recognition has become "superhuman", producing more accurate results than human contestants. This first occurred in 2011 in recognition of traffic signs, and in 2014, with recognition of human faces.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-:7-186>)[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-surpass1-187>)
Deep learning-trained vehicles now interpret 360° camera views.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/<#cite_note-188>) Another example is Facial Dysmorphology Novel Analysis (FDNA) used to analyze cases of human malformation connected to a large database of genetic syndromes. 
### Visual art processing
[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/</w/index.php?title=Deep_learning&action=edit&section=15> "Edit section: Visual art processing")]
